#PROJECT
PR:PROJECT_TITLE                 	Comparing identified and statistically significant lipids and polar metabolites
PR:PROJECT_TITLE                 	in 15-year old serum and dried blood spot samples for longitudinal studies
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	The use of dried blood spots (DBS) has many advantages over traditional plasma
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	and serum samples such as the smaller blood volume required, storage at room
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	temperature, and ability to sample in remote locations. However, understanding
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	the robustness of different analytes in DBS samples is essential, especially in
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	older samples collected for longitudinal studies. Here we analyzed the stability
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	of polar metabolites and lipids in DBS samples collected in 2000-2001 and stored
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	at room temperature. The identified and statistically significant molecules were
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	then compared to matched serum samples stored at –80°C to determine if the
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	DBS samples could be effectively used in a longitudinal study following
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	metabolic disease. A total of 400 polar metabolites and lipids were identified
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	in the serum and DBS samples using gas chromatograph/mass spectrometry (GC/MS),
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	liquid chromatography (LC)/MS, and LC/ion mobility spectrometry-MS (LC/IMS-MS).
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	The identified polar metabolites overlapped well between the sample types,
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	though only one statistically significant metabolite was conserved in a
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	case-control study of older diabetic males with low amounts of high-density
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	lipoproteins and high body mass indices, triacylglycerides and glucose levels
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	when compared to non-diabetic patients with normal levels, indicating that
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	degradation in the DBS samples affects polar metabolite quantitation.
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	Differences in the lipid identifications indicated that some oxidation occurs in
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	the DBS samples. However, 36 statistically significant lipids correlated in both
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	sample types. The difference in the number of statistically significant polar
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	metabolites and lipids indicated that the lipids did not degrade to as great of
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	a degree as the polar metabolites in the DBS samples and lipid quantitation was
PR:PROJECT_SUMMARY               	still possible.
PR:INSTITUTE                     	Pacific Northwest National Laboratory
PR:LAST_NAME                     	Casey

#STUDY
ST:STUDY_TITLE                   	Comparing identified and statistically significant lipids and polar metabolites
ST:STUDY_TITLE                   	in 15-year old serum and dried blood spot samples for longitudinal studies
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	The use of dried blood spots (DBS) has many advantages over traditional plasma
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	and serum samples such as the smaller blood volume required, storage at room
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	temperature, and ability to sample in remote locations. However, understanding
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	the robustness of different analytes in DBS samples is essential, especially in
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	older samples collected for longitudinal studies. Here we analyzed the stability
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	of polar metabolites and lipids in DBS samples collected in 2000-2001 and stored
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	at room temperature. The identified and statistically significant molecules were
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	then compared to matched serum samples stored at –80°C to determine if the
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	DBS samples could be effectively used in a longitudinal study following
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	metabolic disease. A total of 400 polar metabolites and lipids were identified
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	in the serum and DBS samples using gas chromatograph/mass spectrometry (GC/MS),
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	liquid chromatography (LC)/MS, and LC/ion mobility spectrometry-MS (LC/IMS-MS).
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	The identified polar metabolites overlapped well between the sample types,
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	though only one statistically significant metabolite was conserved in a
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	case-control study of older diabetic males with low amounts of high-density
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	lipoproteins and high body mass indices, triacylglycerides and glucose levels
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	when compared to non-diabetic patients with normal levels, indicating that
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	degradation in the DBS samples affects polar metabolite quantitation.
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	Differences in the lipid identifications indicated that some oxidation occurs in
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	the DBS samples. However, 36 statistically significant lipids correlated in both
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	sample types. The difference in the number of statistically significant polar
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	metabolites and lipids indicated that the lipids did not degrade to as great of
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	a degree as the polar metabolites in the DBS samples and lipid quantitation was
ST:STUDY_SUMMARY                 	still possible.

#COLLECTION
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	Matched DBS and serum samples were simultaneously collected in 2000–2001 from
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	10 overnight fasted male participants having an average age of 75.5 ± 6.15
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	years. The institutional review boards at the participating institutions
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	approved the protocol and the study participants provided informed consent. Case
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	participants included 5 diabetic men with high BMIs, TGs and glucose levels, and
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	low amounts of HDL. The 5 control participants had normal metabolic profiles
CO:COLLECTION_SUMMARY            	(normal BMIs, TGs, HDL and glucose levels).
CO:STORAGE_CONDITIONS            	The DBS were stored at room temperature in the dark until analysis, while the
CO:STORAGE_CONDITIONS            	serum was stored at –80°C.
#TREATMENT
TR:TREATMENT_SUMMARY             	Samples collected in 2000–2001 were stored until June 2015.
#SAMPLEPREP
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	For the serum samples, 10 mL of whole blood was drawn and centrifuged so the
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	serum could be extracted. For the DBS samples, 500 μL of blood was collected
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	and 125 μL was blotted in four areas on a Whatman FTA Classic Card. Polar
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	metabolites and lipid extracts were derived from the same sample type (DBS or
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	serum) using a modified Folch extraction in June 2015 and analyzed with MS
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	shortly after. The DBS punch from each patient was transferred into to a 2.0 mL
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	tube where 50 μL of water and then 1200 μL of –20°C 2:1 chloroform/methanol
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	were added. Each sample was vortexed for 30 s then transferred into a shaker at
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	22°C for 60 min at 600 rpm. The samples were vortexed again for 30 s and then
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	250 μL of water was added to induce a phase separation. The sample was gently
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	inverted several times, placed at room temperature for 5 min and then
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	centrifuged at 10,000 g for 5 min at 4°C and put on ice to maintain the clear
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	phase separation. Finally, 400 μL of the top polar layer was removed, dried in
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	a Speedvac, and stored at –80°C for analysis of polar metabolites, while 700
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	μL of the bottom nonpolar layer was removed, dried in a Speedvac, and stored at
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	–20°C in 250 μL of 2:1 chloroform/methanol for lipid analyses. Serum lipids
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	were extracted using a similar procedure except 25 μL of serum was used and
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	then 600 μL of –20°C 2:1 chloroform/methanol was added. After vortexing and
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	shaking, 125 μL of water was added to induce a phase separation and 200 μL of
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	the top polar layer and 350 μL of the bottom nonpolar lipid layer were removed
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	and stored as outlined above. Prior to analysis, the total lipid extracts were
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	dried down and then reconstituted in 70 μL and 100 μL of MeOH for the DBS and
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	serum samples, respectively. To generate pooled case and control samples for
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	LC/MS/MS and LC/IMS-MS analyses, 5 μL aliquots from each reconstituted DBS and
SP:SAMPLEPREP_SUMMARY            	serum sample were removed and combined.
